<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Animal husbandry</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Management, selective breeding, and care of farm animals by humans</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For the dice game, see <a href="/wiki/Animal_Husbandry_(game)" title="Animal Husbandry (game)">Animal Husbandry (game)</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cattle_Feedlot_near_Rocky_Ford,_CO_IMG_5651-2.jpg"><img alt="Cattle feedlot" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2814" data-file-width="3858" decoding="async" height="219" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Cattle_Feedlot_near_Rocky_Ford%2C_CO_IMG_5651-2.jpg/300px-Cattle_Feedlot_near_Rocky_Ford%2C_CO_IMG_5651-2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Cattle_Feedlot_near_Rocky_Ford%2C_CO_IMG_5651-2.jpg/450px-Cattle_Feedlot_near_Rocky_Ford%2C_CO_IMG_5651-2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Cattle_Feedlot_near_Rocky_Ford%2C_CO_IMG_5651-2.jpg/600px-Cattle_Feedlot_near_Rocky_Ford%2C_CO_IMG_5651-2.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cattle_Feedlot_near_Rocky_Ford,_CO_IMG_5651-2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Cattle feedlot in <a href="/wiki/Colorado" title="Colorado">Colorado</a>, United States</div></div></div>
<p><b>Animal husbandry</b> is the branch of <a href="/wiki/Agriculture" title="Agriculture">agriculture</a> concerned with <a href="/wiki/Animal" title="Animal">animals</a> that are raised for <a href="/wiki/Meat" title="Meat">meat</a>, <a href="/wiki/Animal_fiber" title="Animal fiber">fibre</a>, <a href="/wiki/Milk" title="Milk">milk</a>, or other products. It includes day-to-day care, <a href="/wiki/Selective_breeding" title="Selective breeding">selective breeding</a>, and the raising of <a href="/wiki/Livestock" title="Livestock">livestock</a>. Husbandry has a long history, starting with the <a href="/wiki/Neolithic_Revolution" title="Neolithic Revolution">Neolithic Revolution</a> when animals were first <a href="/wiki/Domestication" title="Domestication">domesticated</a>, from around 13,000 BC onwards, predating farming of the <a href="/wiki/History_of_agriculture" title="History of agriculture">first crops</a>. By the time of early civilisations such as <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">ancient Egypt</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cattle" title="Cattle">cattle</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sheep" title="Sheep">sheep</a>, <a href="/wiki/Goat" title="Goat">goats</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Pig" title="Pig">pigs</a> were being raised on farms.
</p><p>Major changes took place in the <a href="/wiki/Columbian_exchange" title="Columbian exchange">Columbian exchange</a>, when Old World livestock were brought to the New World, and then in the <a href="/wiki/British_Agricultural_Revolution" title="British Agricultural Revolution">British Agricultural Revolution</a> of the 18th century, when livestock breeds like the <a href="/wiki/English_Longhorn" title="English Longhorn">Dishley Longhorn</a> cattle and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lincoln_(sheep)" title="Lincoln (sheep)">Lincoln Longwool</a> sheep were rapidly improved by agriculturalists, such as <a href="/wiki/Robert_Bakewell_(agriculturalist)" title="Robert Bakewell (agriculturalist)">Robert Bakewell</a>, to yield more meat, milk, and <a href="/wiki/Wool" title="Wool">wool</a>. A wide range of other species, such as <a href="/wiki/Horse" title="Horse">horse</a>, <a href="/wiki/Water_buffalo" title="Water buffalo">water buffalo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Llama" title="Llama">llama</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rabbit" title="Rabbit">rabbit</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Guinea_pig" title="Guinea pig">guinea pig</a>, are used as livestock in some parts of the world. <a href="/wiki/Insect_farming" title="Insect farming">Insect farming</a>, as well as <a href="/wiki/Aquaculture" title="Aquaculture">aquaculture</a> of <a href="/wiki/Fish" title="Fish">fish</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mollusc" title="Mollusc">molluscs</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Crustacean" title="Crustacean">crustaceans</a>, is widespread. Modern animal husbandry relies on <a href="/wiki/Agriculture#Livestock_production_systems" title="Agriculture">production systems</a> adapted to the type of land available. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Subsistence_farming" title="Subsistence farming">Subsistence farming</a> is being superseded by <a href="/wiki/Intensive_animal_farming" title="Intensive animal farming">intensive animal farming</a> in the more developed parts of the world, where, for example, beef cattle are kept in high density <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Feedlots" title="Feedlots">feedlots</a>, and thousands of <a href="/wiki/Chicken" title="Chicken">chickens</a> may be raised in broiler houses or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Battery_farm" title="Battery farm">batteries</a>. On poorer soil, such as in uplands, animals are often kept more extensively and may be allowed to roam widely, foraging for themselves.
</p><p>Most livestock are <a href="/wiki/Herbivore" title="Herbivore">herbivores</a>, except for pigs and chickens which are <a href="/wiki/Omnivore" title="Omnivore">omnivores</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ruminantia" title="Ruminantia">Ruminants</a> like cattle and sheep are adapted to feed on grass; they can forage outdoors or may be fed entirely or in part on rations richer in energy and protein, such as pelleted cereals. Pigs and poultry cannot digest the <a href="/wiki/Cellulose" title="Cellulose">cellulose</a> in forage and require other high-protein foods.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Animal_husbandry&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The verb <i>to husband</i>, meaning "to manage carefully," derives from an older meaning of <i><a href="/wiki/Husband" title="Husband">husband</a></i>, which in the 14th century referred to the ownership and care of a household or farm, but today means the "control or judicious use of resources," and in agriculture, the cultivation of plants or animals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Farmer" title="Farmer">Farmers</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rancher" title="Rancher">ranchers</a> who raise livestock are considered to practice <i>animal husbandry</i>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Animal_husbandry&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/History_of_agriculture" title="History of agriculture">History of agriculture</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Birth_of_husbandry">Birth of husbandry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Animal_husbandry&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Birth of husbandry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Neolithic_Revolution" title="Neolithic Revolution">Neolithic Revolution</a> and <a href="/wiki/Domestication_of_animals" title="Domestication of animals">Domestication of animals</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Fat_tailed_sheep,_Afghanistan,_1976.jpg"><img alt="Fat-tailed sheep in Afghanistan" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1076" data-file-width="1620" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Fat_tailed_sheep%2C_Afghanistan%2C_1976.jpg/220px-Fat_tailed_sheep%2C_Afghanistan%2C_1976.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Fat_tailed_sheep%2C_Afghanistan%2C_1976.jpg/330px-Fat_tailed_sheep%2C_Afghanistan%2C_1976.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Fat_tailed_sheep%2C_Afghanistan%2C_1976.jpg/440px-Fat_tailed_sheep%2C_Afghanistan%2C_1976.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Fat_tailed_sheep,_Afghanistan,_1976.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The domestication of ruminants, like these <a href="/wiki/Fat-tailed_sheep" title="Fat-tailed sheep">fat-tailed sheep</a> in Afghanistan, provided <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pastoral_nomad" title="Pastoral nomad">nomads</a> across the Middle East and central Asia with a reliable source of food.</div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Domestication" title="Domestication">domestication</a> of livestock was driven by the need to have food on hand when hunting was unproductive. The desirable characteristics of a domestic animal are that it should be useful to the domesticator, should be able to thrive in his or her company, should breed freely, and be easy to tend.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clutton-Brock_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-Clutton-Brock-2">[2]</a></sup> Domestication was not a single event, but a process repeated at various periods in different places. Sheep and goats were the animals that accompanied the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pastoral_nomad" title="Pastoral nomad">nomads</a> in the Middle East, while cattle and pigs were associated with more settled communities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Historyworld_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-Historyworld-3">[3]</a></sup> The first wild animal to be domesticated was the <a href="/wiki/Dog" title="Dog">dog</a>. Half-wild dogs, perhaps starting with young individuals, may have been tolerated as scavengers and killers of vermin, and being naturally <a href="/wiki/Pack_hunter" title="Pack hunter">pack hunters</a>, were predisposed to become part of the human pack and join in the hunt. Prey animals, sheep, goats, pigs and cattle, were progressively domesticated early in the history of agriculture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Historyworld_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-Historyworld-3">[3]</a></sup> Pigs were domesticated in the <a href="/wiki/Near_East" title="Near East">Near East</a> between 8,500 and 8000 BC,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> sheep and goats in or near the <a href="/wiki/Fertile_Crescent" title="Fertile Crescent">Fertile Crescent</a> about 8,500 BC,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> and cattle from wild <a href="/wiki/Aurochs" title="Aurochs">aurochs</a> in the areas of modern Turkey and Pakistan around 8,500 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McTavish_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-McTavish-6">[6]</a></sup> A cow was a great advantage to a villager as she produced more milk than her calf needed, and her strength could be put to use as a <a href="/wiki/Working_animal" title="Working animal">working animal</a>, pulling a plough to increase production of crops, and drawing a sledge, and later a cart, to bring the produce home from the field. Draught animals were first used about 4,000 BC in the Middle East, increasing agricultural production immeasurably.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Historyworld_3-2"><a href="#cite_note-Historyworld-3">[3]</a></sup>
</p><p>In southern Asia, the <a href="/wiki/Elephant" title="Elephant">elephant</a> was domesticated by 6,000 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gupta_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-gupta-7">[7]</a></sup> Fossilised chicken bones dated to 5040 BC have been found in northeastern China, far from where their wild ancestors lived in the jungles of tropical Asia, but archaeologists believe that the original purpose of domestication was for the sport of cockfighting.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> Meanwhile, in South America, the <a href="/wiki/Llama" title="Llama">llama</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Alpaca" title="Alpaca">alpaca</a> had been domesticated, probably before 3,000 BC, as beasts of burden and for their wool. Neither was strong enough to pull a plough which limited the development of agriculture in the New World.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Historyworld_3-3"><a href="#cite_note-Historyworld-3">[3]</a></sup> Horses occur naturally on the steppes of Central Asia and their domestication began around 3,000 BC in the <a href="/wiki/Black_Sea" title="Black Sea">Black Sea</a> and <a href="/wiki/Caspian_Sea" title="Caspian Sea">Caspian Sea</a> region. Although horses were originally seen as a source of meat, their use as <a href="/wiki/Pack_animal" title="Pack animal">pack animals</a> and for riding followed. Around the same time, the <a href="/wiki/Nubian_wild_ass" title="Nubian wild ass">wild ass</a> was being tamed in Egypt. <a href="/wiki/Camel" title="Camel">Camels</a> were domesticated soon after this,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sapir-Hen_2013_277–285_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sapir-Hen_2013_277–285-9">[9]</a></sup> with the <a href="/wiki/Bactrian_camel" title="Bactrian camel">Bactrian camel</a> in <a href="/wiki/Mongolia" title="Mongolia">Mongolia</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabian_camel" title="Arabian camel">Arabian camel</a> becoming beasts of burden. By 1000 BC, caravans of Arabian camels were linking India with Mesopotamia and the Mediterranean.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Historyworld_3-4"><a href="#cite_note-Historyworld-3">[3]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ancient_civilisations">Ancient civilisations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Animal_husbandry&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Ancient civilisations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Egyptian_Domesticated_Animals.jpg"><img alt="Egyptian hieroglyphic of cattle" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1800" data-file-width="3200" decoding="async" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Egyptian_Domesticated_Animals.jpg/220px-Egyptian_Domesticated_Animals.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Egyptian_Domesticated_Animals.jpg/330px-Egyptian_Domesticated_Animals.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Egyptian_Domesticated_Animals.jpg/440px-Egyptian_Domesticated_Animals.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Egyptian_Domesticated_Animals.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Milking cattle in <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">ancient Egypt</a></div></div></div>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">ancient Egypt</a>, cattle were the most important livestock, and sheep, goats, and pigs were also kept; poultry including ducks, geese, and pigeons were captured in nets and bred on farms, where they were force-fed with dough to fatten them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> The Nile provided a plentiful source of fish. <a href="/wiki/Honey_bee" title="Honey bee">Honey bees</a> were domesticated from at least the Old Kingdom, providing both honey and wax.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">ancient Rome</a>, all the livestock known in ancient Egypt were available. In addition, rabbits were domesticated for food by the first century BC. To help flush them out from their burrows, the <a href="/wiki/Polecat" title="Polecat">polecat</a> was domesticated as the <a href="/wiki/Ferret" title="Ferret">ferret</a>, its use described by <a href="/wiki/Pliny_the_Elder" title="Pliny the Elder">Pliny the Elder</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Medieval_husbandry">Medieval husbandry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Animal_husbandry&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Medieval husbandry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sheep_in_pen_medieval_France_15th_century_MS_Douce_195.jpg"><img alt="Painting of shepherd with sheep" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="556" data-file-width="736" decoding="async" height="166" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Sheep_in_pen_medieval_France_15th_century_MS_Douce_195.jpg/220px-Sheep_in_pen_medieval_France_15th_century_MS_Douce_195.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Sheep_in_pen_medieval_France_15th_century_MS_Douce_195.jpg/330px-Sheep_in_pen_medieval_France_15th_century_MS_Douce_195.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Sheep_in_pen_medieval_France_15th_century_MS_Douce_195.jpg/440px-Sheep_in_pen_medieval_France_15th_century_MS_Douce_195.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sheep_in_pen_medieval_France_15th_century_MS_Douce_195.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Shepherd with sheep in woven hurdle pen. Medieval France. 15th century, MS Douce 195</div></div></div>
<p>In northern Europe, agriculture including animal husbandry went into decline when the Roman empire collapsed. Some aspects such as the herding of animals continued throughout the period. By the 11th century, the economy had recovered and the countryside was again productive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> The <i><a href="/wiki/Domesday_Book" title="Domesday Book">Domesday Book</a></i> recorded every parcel of land and every animal in England: "there was not one single hide, nor a yard of land, nay, moreover ... not even an ox, nor a cow, nor a swine was there left, that was not set down in [the king's] writ."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> For example, the royal <a href="/wiki/Manorialism" title="Manorialism">manor</a> of <a href="/wiki/Earley" title="Earley">Earley</a> in <a href="/wiki/Berkshire" title="Berkshire">Berkshire</a>, one of thousands of villages recorded in the book, had in 1086 "2 fisheries worth [paying tax of] 7s and 6d [each year] and 20 acres of meadow [for livestock]. Woodland for [feeding] 70 pigs."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> The improvements of animal husbandry in the medieval period in Europe went hand in hand with other developments. Improvements to the plough allowed the soil to be tilled to a greater depth. Horses took over from oxen as the main providers of traction, new ideas on <a href="/wiki/Crop_rotation" title="Crop rotation">crop rotation</a> were developed and the growing of crops for winter fodder gained ground.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> Peas, beans and vetches became common; they increased soil fertility through nitrogen fixation, allowing more livestock to be kept.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Campbell_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Campbell-17">[17]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Columbian_exchange">Columbian exchange</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Animal_husbandry&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Columbian exchange">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Columbian_exchange" title="Columbian exchange">Columbian exchange</a></div>
<p>Exploration and colonisation of North and South America resulted in the introduction into Europe of such crops as maize, potatoes, sweet potatoes and manioc, while the principal Old World livestock – cattle, horses, sheep and goats – were introduced into the New World for the first time along with wheat, barley, rice and turnips.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Agricultural_Revolution">Agricultural Revolution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Animal_husbandry&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Agricultural Revolution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/British_Agricultural_Revolution" title="British Agricultural Revolution">British Agricultural Revolution</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lincoln_Longwool_Lamb.jpg"><img alt="Lincoln Longwool Sheep" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="640" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Lincoln_Longwool_Lamb.jpg/220px-Lincoln_Longwool_Lamb.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Lincoln_Longwool_Lamb.jpg/330px-Lincoln_Longwool_Lamb.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Lincoln_Longwool_Lamb.jpg/440px-Lincoln_Longwool_Lamb.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lincoln_Longwool_Lamb.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Lincoln_sheep" title="Lincoln sheep">Lincoln Longwool</a> breed was improved by <a href="/wiki/Robert_Bakewell_(agriculturalist)" title="Robert Bakewell (agriculturalist)">Robert Bakewell</a> in the 18th century.</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Selective_breeding" title="Selective breeding">Selective breeding</a> for desired traits was established as a scientific practice by <a href="/wiki/Robert_Bakewell_(agriculturalist)" title="Robert Bakewell (agriculturalist)">Robert Bakewell</a> during the <a href="/wiki/British_Agricultural_Revolution" title="British Agricultural Revolution">British Agricultural Revolution</a> in the 18th century. One of his most important breeding programs was with sheep. Using native stock, he was able to quickly select for large, yet fine-boned sheep, with long, lustrous wool. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lincoln_(sheep)" title="Lincoln (sheep)">Lincoln Longwool</a> was improved by Bakewell and in turn the Lincoln was used to develop the subsequent breed, named the New (or Dishley) Leicester. It was <a href="/wiki/Polled_livestock" title="Polled livestock">hornless</a> and had a square, meaty body with straight top lines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> These sheep were exported widely and have contributed to numerous modern breeds. Under his influence, English farmers began to breed <a href="/wiki/Cattle" title="Cattle">cattle</a> for use primarily as beef. Long-horned heifers were crossed with the Westmoreland bull to create the <a href="/wiki/English_Longhorn" title="English Longhorn">Dishley Longhorn</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup>
</p><p>The semi-natural, unfertilised pastures formed by traditional agricultural methods in Europe were managed by grazing and mowing. As the ecological impact of this land management strategy is similar to the impact of such natural disturbances as a <a href="/wiki/Wildfire" title="Wildfire">wildfire</a>, this agricultural system shares many beneficial characteristics with a natural habitat, including the promotion of <a href="/wiki/Biodiversity" title="Biodiversity">biodiversity</a>. This strategy is declining in Europe today due to the intensification of agriculture. The mechanized and chemical methods used are causing biodiversity to decline.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pykala2000_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pykala2000-21">[21]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Practices">Practices</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Animal_husbandry&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Practices">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Livestock" title="Livestock">Livestock</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Systems">Systems</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Animal_husbandry&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Systems">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Agriculture#Livestock_production_systems" title="Agriculture">Agriculture § Livestock production systems</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Intensive_animal_farming" title="Intensive animal farming">Intensive animal farming</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Herdwicks_Lake_District.jpg"><img alt="Herdwick sheep" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="375" data-file-width="500" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Herdwicks_Lake_District.jpg/220px-Herdwicks_Lake_District.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Herdwicks_Lake_District.jpg/330px-Herdwicks_Lake_District.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Herdwicks_Lake_District.jpg/440px-Herdwicks_Lake_District.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Herdwicks_Lake_District.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Herdwick_sheep" title="Herdwick sheep">Herdwick sheep</a> in an extensive <a href="/wiki/Hill_farming" title="Hill farming">hill farming</a> system, <a href="/wiki/Lake_District" title="Lake District">Lake District</a>, England</div></div></div>
<p>Traditionally, animal husbandry was part of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Subsistence_farming" title="Subsistence farming">subsistence farmer's</a> way of life, producing not only the food needed by the family but also the fuel, fertiliser, clothing, transport and draught power. Killing the animal for food was a secondary consideration, and wherever possible its products such as wool, eggs, milk and blood (by the <a href="/wiki/Maasai_people" title="Maasai people">Maasai</a>) were harvested while the animal was still alive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Webster_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Webster-22">[22]</a></sup> In the traditional system of <a href="/wiki/Transhumance" title="Transhumance">transhumance</a>, people and livestock moved seasonally between fixed summer and winter pastures; in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Montane" title="Montane">montane</a> regions the summer pasture was up in the mountains, the winter pasture in the valleys.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup>
</p><p>Animals can be kept extensively or intensively. Extensive systems involve animals roaming at will, or under the supervision of a herdsman, often for their protection from <a href="/wiki/Predation" title="Predation">predators</a>. <a href="/wiki/Ranch" title="Ranch">Ranching</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Western_United_States" title="Western United States">Western United States</a> involves large herds of cattle grazing widely over public and private lands.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Starrs_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-Starrs-24">[24]</a></sup> Similar cattle stations are found in South America, Australia and other places with large areas of land and low rainfall. Ranching systems have been used for <a href="/wiki/Sheep" title="Sheep">sheep</a>, <a href="/wiki/Deer" title="Deer">deer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ostrich" title="Ostrich">ostrich</a>, <a href="/wiki/Emu" title="Emu">emu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Llama" title="Llama">llama</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alpaca" title="Alpaca">alpaca</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Levinson_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-Levinson-25">[25]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the uplands of the United Kingdom, sheep are turned out on the fells in spring and graze the abundant mountain grasses untended, being brought to lower altitudes late in the year, with supplementary feeding being provided in winter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> In rural locations, <a href="/wiki/Pig" title="Pig">pigs</a> and <a href="/wiki/Poultry" title="Poultry">poultry</a> can obtain much of their nutrition from scavenging, and in African communities, hens may live for months without being fed, and still produce one or two eggs a week.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Webster_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-Webster-22">[22]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hog_confinement_barn_interior.jpg"><img alt="Pigs in a barn" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="771" data-file-width="1079" decoding="async" height="157" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Hog_confinement_barn_interior.jpg/220px-Hog_confinement_barn_interior.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Hog_confinement_barn_interior.jpg/330px-Hog_confinement_barn_interior.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Hog_confinement_barn_interior.jpg/440px-Hog_confinement_barn_interior.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hog_confinement_barn_interior.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Pigs in an <a href="/wiki/Intensive_animal_farming" title="Intensive animal farming">intensive system</a>, <a href="/wiki/Midwestern_United_States" title="Midwestern United States">Midwestern United States</a></div></div></div>
<p>At the other extreme, in the more developed parts of the world, <a href="/wiki/Intensive_animal_farming" title="Intensive animal farming">animals are often intensively managed</a>; dairy cows may be kept in zero-grazing conditions with all their forage brought to them; beef cattle may be kept in high density <a href="/wiki/Feedlot" title="Feedlot">feedlots</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> pigs may be housed in climate-controlled buildings and never go outdoors;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> poultry may be reared in barns and kept in cages as laying birds under lighting-controlled conditions. In between these two extremes are semi-intensive, often family-run farms where livestock graze outside for much of the year, silage or hay is made to cover the times of year when the grass stops growing, and fertiliser, feed, and other inputs are brought onto the farm from outside.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blount_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-Blount-29">[29]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Feeding">Feeding</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Animal_husbandry&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Feeding">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Animal_feed" title="Animal feed">animal feed</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cattle_round_a_feeder_with_Avon_Farm_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1563342.jpg"><img alt="Cattle around an outdoor feeder" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="538" data-file-width="640" decoding="async" height="185" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Cattle_round_a_feeder_with_Avon_Farm_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1563342.jpg/220px-Cattle_round_a_feeder_with_Avon_Farm_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1563342.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Cattle_round_a_feeder_with_Avon_Farm_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1563342.jpg/330px-Cattle_round_a_feeder_with_Avon_Farm_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1563342.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Cattle_round_a_feeder_with_Avon_Farm_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1563342.jpg/440px-Cattle_round_a_feeder_with_Avon_Farm_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1563342.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cattle_round_a_feeder_with_Avon_Farm_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1563342.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Cattle" title="Cattle">Cattle</a> around an outdoor feeder</div></div></div>
<p>Animals used as livestock are predominantly <a href="/wiki/Herbivore" title="Herbivore">herbivorous</a>, the main exceptions being the pig and the chicken which are omnivorous. The herbivores can be divided into "concentrate selectors" which selectively feed on seeds, fruits and highly nutritious young foliage, "grazers" which mainly feed on grass, and "intermediate feeders" which choose their diet from the whole range of available plant material. Cattle, sheep, goats, deer and antelopes are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ruminantia" title="Ruminantia">ruminants</a>; they digest food in two steps, chewing and swallowing in the normal way, and then regurgitating the semidigested cud to chew it again and thus extract the maximum possible food value.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dryden_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dryden-30">[30]</a></sup>
The dietary needs of these animals is mostly met by eating grass. Grasses grow from the base of the leaf-blade, enabling it to thrive even when heavily grazed or cut.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup>
</p><p>In many climates grass growth is seasonal, for example in the temperate <a href="/wiki/Summer" title="Summer">summer</a> or tropical <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rainy_season" title="Rainy season">rainy season</a>, so some areas of the crop are set aside to be cut and preserved, either as <a href="/wiki/Hay" title="Hay">hay</a> (dried grass), or as <a href="/wiki/Silage" title="Silage">silage</a> (fermented grass).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> Other forage crops are also grown and many of these, as well as crop residues, can be ensiled to fill the gap in the nutritional needs of livestock in the lean season.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:LeinPresskuchen-CBIO.jpg"><img alt="Cattle feed pellets" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="730" data-file-width="1000" decoding="async" height="161" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/LeinPresskuchen-CBIO.jpg/220px-LeinPresskuchen-CBIO.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/LeinPresskuchen-CBIO.jpg/330px-LeinPresskuchen-CBIO.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/LeinPresskuchen-CBIO.jpg/440px-LeinPresskuchen-CBIO.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:LeinPresskuchen-CBIO.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Cattle feed pellets of pressed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Linseed" title="Linseed">linseed</a></div></div></div>
<p>Extensively reared animals may subsist entirely on forage, but more intensively kept livestock will require energy and protein-rich foods in addition. Energy is mainly derived from cereals and cereal by-products, fats and oils and sugar-rich foods, while protein may come from fish or meat meal, milk products, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Legumes" title="Legumes">legumes</a> and other plant foods, often the by-products of vegetable oil extraction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dryden2008_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dryden2008-34">[34]</a></sup>
Pigs and poultry are non-ruminants and unable to digest the <a href="/wiki/Cellulose" title="Cellulose">cellulose</a> in grass and other forages, so they are fed entirely on cereals and other high-energy foodstuffs. The ingredients for the animals' rations can be grown on the farm or can be bought, in the form of pelleted or cubed, compound foodstuffs specially formulated for the different classes of livestock, their growth stages and their specific nutritional requirements. Vitamins and minerals are added to balance the diet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FSA_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-FSA-35">[35]</a></sup> Farmed fish are usually fed pelleted food.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FSA_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-FSA-35">[35]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Breeding">Breeding</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Animal_husbandry&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Breeding">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Animal_breeding" title="Animal breeding">Animal breeding</a></div>
<p>The breeding of farm animals seldom occurs spontaneously but is managed by farmers with a view to encouraging traits seen as desirable. These include hardiness, fertility, docility, mothering abilities, fast growth rates, low feed consumption per unit of growth, better body proportions, higher yields, and better fibre qualities. Undesirable traits such as health defects and aggressiveness are selected against.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Turner_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-Turner-36">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup>
</p><p>Selective breeding has been responsible for large increases in productivity. For example, in 2007, a typical broiler chicken at eight weeks old was 4.8 times as heavy as a bird of similar age in 1957,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Turner_36-1"><a href="#cite_note-Turner-36">[36]</a></sup> while in the thirty years to 2007, the average milk yield of a dairy cow in the United States nearly doubled.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Turner_36-2"><a href="#cite_note-Turner-36">[36]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Animal_health">Animal health</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Animal_husbandry&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Animal health">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Veterinary_medicine" title="Veterinary medicine">Veterinary medicine</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Vaccination_of_goat_in_Niger.jpg"><img alt="Vaccination of a goat" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="768" data-file-width="1024" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Vaccination_of_goat_in_Niger.jpg/220px-Vaccination_of_goat_in_Niger.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Vaccination_of_goat_in_Niger.jpg/330px-Vaccination_of_goat_in_Niger.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Vaccination_of_goat_in_Niger.jpg/440px-Vaccination_of_goat_in_Niger.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Vaccination_of_goat_in_Niger.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Vaccination" title="Vaccination">Vaccinating</a> a <a href="/wiki/Goat" title="Goat">goat</a>, <a href="/wiki/Niger" title="Niger">Niger</a></div></div></div>
<p>Good husbandry, proper feeding, and <a href="/wiki/Hygiene" title="Hygiene">hygiene</a> are the main contributors to animal health on the farm, bringing economic benefits through maximised production. When, despite these precautions, animals still become sick, they are treated with <a href="/wiki/Veterinary_medicine" title="Veterinary medicine">veterinary medicines</a>, by the farmer and the <a href="/wiki/Veterinarian" title="Veterinarian">veterinarian</a>. In the European Union, when farmers treat their own animals, they are required to follow the guidelines for treatment and to record the treatments given.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> Animals are susceptible to a number of diseases and conditions that may affect their health. Some, like <a href="/wiki/Classical_swine_fever" title="Classical swine fever">classical swine fever</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Scrapie" title="Scrapie">scrapie</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> are specific to one type of stock, while others, like <a href="/wiki/Foot-and-mouth_disease" title="Foot-and-mouth disease">foot-and-mouth disease</a> affect all <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cloven-hoofed" title="Cloven-hoofed">cloven-hoofed</a> animals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> Animals living under intensive conditions are prone to internal and external <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parasite" title="Parasite">parasites</a>; increasing numbers of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sea_lice" title="Sea lice">sea lice</a> are affecting farmed salmon in Scotland.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> Reducing the parasite burdens of livestock results in increased productivity and profitability.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup>
</p><p>Where the condition is serious, governments impose regulations on import and export, on the movement of stock, <a href="/wiki/Quarantine" title="Quarantine">quarantine</a> restrictions and the reporting of suspected cases. <a href="/wiki/Vaccine" title="Vaccine">Vaccines</a> are available against certain diseases, and antibiotics are widely used where appropriate. At one time, antibiotics were routinely added to certain compound foodstuffs to promote growth, but this practice is now frowned on in many countries because of the risk that it may lead to <a href="/wiki/Antimicrobial_resistance" title="Antimicrobial resistance">antimicrobial resistance</a> in livestock and in humans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Farmyard_Drawing.jpg"><img alt="Watercolor drawing of farmyard with cow, horse, pigs, and chickens" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="466" data-file-width="466" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Farmyard_Drawing.jpg/220px-Farmyard_Drawing.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Farmyard_Drawing.jpg/330px-Farmyard_Drawing.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Farmyard_Drawing.jpg/440px-Farmyard_Drawing.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Farmyard_Drawing.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Familiar livestock: ink and watercolour drawing of a farmyard with cow, horse, pigs, and chickens, 1869</div></div></div>
<p>Governments are concerned with <a href="/wiki/Zoonosis" title="Zoonosis">zoonoses</a>, diseases that humans may acquire from animals. Wild animal populations may harbour diseases that can affect domestic animals which may acquire them as a result of insufficient <a href="/wiki/Biosecurity" title="Biosecurity">biosecurity</a>. An outbreak of <a href="/wiki/Nipah_virus" title="Nipah virus">Nipah virus</a> in Malaysia in 1999 was traced back to pigs becoming ill after contact with fruit-eating <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flying_fox" title="Flying fox">flying foxes</a>, their faeces and urine. The pigs in turn passed the infection to humans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chua_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-chua-45">[45]</a></sup> Avian flu <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/H5N1" title="H5N1">H5N1</a> is present in wild bird populations and can be carried large distances by migrating birds. This virus is easily transmissible to domestic poultry, and to humans living in close proximity with them. Other infectious diseases affecting wild animals, farm animals and humans include <a href="/wiki/Rabies" title="Rabies">rabies</a>, <a href="/wiki/Leptospirosis" title="Leptospirosis">leptospirosis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Brucellosis" title="Brucellosis">brucellosis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mycobacterium_bovis" title="Mycobacterium bovis">tuberculosis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Trichinosis" title="Trichinosis">trichinosis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Norrgren_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-Norrgren-46">[46]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Range_of_species">Range of species</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Animal_husbandry&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Range of species">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Livestock#Types" title="Livestock">Livestock § Types</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aquaculture#Species_groups" title="Aquaculture">Aquaculture § Species groups</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Entomophagy" title="Entomophagy">Entomophagy</a></div>
<p>There is no single universally agreed definition of which species are livestock. Widely agreed types of livestock include cattle for beef and dairy, sheep, goats, pigs, and poultry. Various other species are sometimes considered livestock, such as horses,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> while poultry birds are sometimes excluded. In some parts of the world, livestock includes species such as buffalo, and the South American camelids, the alpaca and llama.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> Some authorities use much broader definitions to include fish in <a href="/wiki/Aquaculture" title="Aquaculture">aquaculture</a>, micro-livestock such as <a href="/wiki/Cuniculture" title="Cuniculture">rabbits</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rodent_farming" title="Rodent farming">rodents</a> like <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Guinea_pigs" title="Guinea pigs">guinea pigs</a>, as well as insects from <a href="/wiki/Honey_bee" title="Honey bee">honey bees</a> to <a href="/wiki/Cricket_(insect)#As_food" title="Cricket (insect)">crickets raised for human consumption</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Merino_shearing.jpg"><img alt="Sheering merino sheep" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="263" data-file-width="350" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Merino_shearing.jpg/220px-Merino_shearing.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Merino_shearing.jpg/330px-Merino_shearing.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/fa/Merino_shearing.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Merino_shearing.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shearing" title="Shearing">Shearing</a> a <a href="/wiki/Merino" title="Merino">Merino</a> sheep for its <a href="/wiki/Wool" title="Wool">wool</a></div></div></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Products">Products</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Animal_husbandry&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Products">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Animal_product" title="Animal product">Animal product</a></div>
<p>Animals are raised for a wide variety of products, principally <a href="/wiki/Meat" title="Meat">meat</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wool" title="Wool">wool</a>, <a href="/wiki/Milk" title="Milk">milk</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Egg" title="Egg">eggs</a>, but also including <a href="/wiki/Tallow" title="Tallow">tallow</a>, <a href="/wiki/Isinglass" title="Isinglass">isinglass</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rennet" title="Rennet">rennet</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> Animals are also kept for more specialised purposes, such as to produce <a href="/wiki/Vaccine" title="Vaccine">vaccines</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Antiserum" title="Antiserum">antiserum</a> (containing <a href="/wiki/Antibody" title="Antibody">antibodies</a>) for medical use.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> Where fodder or other crops are grown alongside animals, <a href="/wiki/Manure" title="Manure">manure</a> can serve as a fertiliser, returning minerals and organic matter to the soil in a semi-closed organic system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Branches">Branches</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Animal_husbandry&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Branches">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dairy">Dairy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Animal_husbandry&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Dairy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Dairy_farming" title="Dairy farming">Dairy farming</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:2014-07-25_Melkkarussel_-_Hemme_Milch_(5).jpg"><img alt="Rotary milking parlour" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3456" data-file-width="5184" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/2014-07-25_Melkkarussel_-_Hemme_Milch_%285%29.jpg/220px-2014-07-25_Melkkarussel_-_Hemme_Milch_%285%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/2014-07-25_Melkkarussel_-_Hemme_Milch_%285%29.jpg/330px-2014-07-25_Melkkarussel_-_Hemme_Milch_%285%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/2014-07-25_Melkkarussel_-_Hemme_Milch_%285%29.jpg/440px-2014-07-25_Melkkarussel_-_Hemme_Milch_%285%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:2014-07-25_Melkkarussel_-_Hemme_Milch_(5).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A modern rotary milking parlour, Germany</div></div></div>
<p>Although all mammals produce milk to nourish their young, the cow is predominantly used throughout the world to produce milk and milk products for human consumption. Other animals used to a lesser extent for this purpose include sheep, goats, camels, buffaloes, yaks, reindeer, horses and donkeys.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FAOdairy_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-FAOdairy-57">[57]</a></sup>
</p><p>All these animals have been domesticated over the centuries, being bred for such desirable characteristics as fecundity, productivity, docility and the ability to thrive under the prevailing conditions. Whereas in the past cattle had multiple functions, modern dairy cow breeding has resulted in specialised <a href="/wiki/Holstein_Friesian_cattle" title="Holstein Friesian cattle">Holstein Friesian-type</a> animals that produce large quantities of milk economically. <a href="/wiki/Artificial_insemination" title="Artificial insemination">Artificial insemination</a> is widely available to allow farmers to select for the particular traits that suit their circumstances.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FAObreeding_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-FAObreeding-58">[58]</a></sup>
</p><p>Whereas in the past cows were kept in small herds on <a href="/wiki/Family_farm" title="Family farm">family farms</a>, grazing pastures and being fed hay in winter, nowadays there is a trend towards larger herds, more intensive systems, the feeding of <a href="/wiki/Silage" title="Silage">silage</a> and "zero grazing", a system where grass is cut and brought to the cow, which is housed year-round.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup>
</p><p>In many communities, milk production is only part of the purpose of keeping an animal which may also be used as a beast of burden or to draw a plough, or for the production of fibre, meat and leather, with the <a href="/wiki/Cow_dung" title="Cow dung">dung</a> being used for fuel or for the improvement of <a href="/wiki/Soil_fertility" title="Soil fertility">soil fertility</a>. Sheep and goats may be favoured for dairy production in climates and conditions that do not suit dairy cows.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FAOdairy_57-1"><a href="#cite_note-FAOdairy-57">[57]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Meat">Meat</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Animal_husbandry&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Meat">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hereford_bull_large.jpg"><img alt="Hereford cow" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="369" data-file-width="550" decoding="async" height="148" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Hereford_bull_large.jpg/220px-Hereford_bull_large.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Hereford_bull_large.jpg/330px-Hereford_bull_large.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Hereford_bull_large.jpg/440px-Hereford_bull_large.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hereford_bull_large.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hereford_(cattle)" title="Hereford (cattle)">Hereford</a> is a hardy breed of <a href="/wiki/Beef_cattle" title="Beef cattle">beef cattle</a>, now raised in many countries around the world.</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Meat_industry" title="Meat industry">Meat industry</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cattle" title="Cattle">Cattle</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sheep_farming" title="Sheep farming">Sheep farming</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pig_farming" title="Pig farming">Pig farming</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Cuniculture" title="Cuniculture">Cuniculture</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Meat" title="Meat">Meat</a>, mainly from farmed animals, is a major source of dietary <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">protein</a> and essential nutrients around the world, averaging about 8% of man's energy intake.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McAfee_McSorley_2010_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-McAfee_McSorley_2010-60">[60]</a></sup> The actual types eaten depend on local preferences, availability, cost and other factors, with cattle, sheep, pigs and goats being the main species involved. Cattle generally produce a single offspring annually which takes more than a year to mature; sheep and goats often have twins and these are ready for slaughter in less than a year; pigs are more prolific, producing more than one litter of up to about 11<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> piglets each year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gregory_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gregory-62">[62]</a></sup> Horses, donkeys, deer, buffalo, llamas, alpacas, guanacos and vicunas are farmed for meat in various regions. Some desirable traits of animals raised for meat include fecundity, hardiness, fast growth rate, ease of management and high food conversion efficiency. About half of the world's meat is produced from animals grazing on open ranges or on enclosed pastures, the other half being produced intensively in various factory-farming systems; these are mostly cows, pigs or poultry, and often reared indoors, typically at high densities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Miller_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-Miller-63">[63]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Poultry">Poultry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Animal_husbandry&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Poultry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Battery_hens_-Bastos,_Sao_Paulo,_Brazil-31March2007.jpg"><img alt="Battery hens" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="482" data-file-width="321" decoding="async" height="255" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Battery_hens_-Bastos%2C_Sao_Paulo%2C_Brazil-31March2007.jpg/170px-Battery_hens_-Bastos%2C_Sao_Paulo%2C_Brazil-31March2007.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Battery_hens_-Bastos%2C_Sao_Paulo%2C_Brazil-31March2007.jpg/255px-Battery_hens_-Bastos%2C_Sao_Paulo%2C_Brazil-31March2007.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/dd/Battery_hens_-Bastos%2C_Sao_Paulo%2C_Brazil-31March2007.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Battery_hens_-Bastos,_Sao_Paulo,_Brazil-31March2007.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Battery hens, Brazil</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Poultry_farming" title="Poultry farming">Poultry farming</a></div>
<p>Poultry, kept for their eggs and for their meat, include chickens, turkeys, geese and ducks. The great majority of laying birds used for egg production are chickens. Methods for keeping layers range from free-range systems, where the birds can roam as they will but are housed at night for their own protection, through semi-intensive systems where they are housed in barns and have perches, litter and some freedom of movement, to intensive systems where they are kept in cages. The <a href="/wiki/Battery_cage" title="Battery cage">battery cages</a> are arranged in long rows in multiple tiers, with external feeders, drinkers, and egg collection facilities. This is the most labour saving and economical method of egg production but has been criticised on animal welfare grounds as the birds are unable to exhibit their normal behaviours.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the developed world, the majority of the poultry reared for meat is raised indoors in big sheds, with automated equipment under environmentally controlled conditions. Chickens raised in this way are known as broilers, and genetic improvements have meant that they can be grown to slaughter weight within six or seven weeks of hatching. Newly hatched chicks are restricted to a small area and given supplementary heating. Litter on the floor absorbs the droppings and the area occupied is expanded as they grow. Feed and water is supplied automatically and the lighting is controlled. The birds may be harvested on several occasions or the whole shed may be cleared at one time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup>
</p><p>A similar rearing system is usually used for turkeys, which are less hardy than chickens, but they take longer to grow and are often moved on to separate fattening units to finish.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> Ducks are particularly popular in Asia and Australia and can be killed at seven weeks under commercial conditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Aquaculture">Aquaculture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Animal_husbandry&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Aquaculture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bassins_de_la_pisciculture_de_la_Calonne.jpg"><img alt="Freshwater fish farm" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3064" data-file-width="4597" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Bassins_de_la_pisciculture_de_la_Calonne.jpg/220px-Bassins_de_la_pisciculture_de_la_Calonne.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Bassins_de_la_pisciculture_de_la_Calonne.jpg/330px-Bassins_de_la_pisciculture_de_la_Calonne.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Bassins_de_la_pisciculture_de_la_Calonne.jpg/440px-Bassins_de_la_pisciculture_de_la_Calonne.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bassins_de_la_pisciculture_de_la_Calonne.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Freshwater <a href="/wiki/Fish_farming" title="Fish farming">fish farming</a>, France</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Aquaculture" title="Aquaculture">Aquaculture</a></div>
<p>Aquaculture has been defined as "the farming of aquatic organisms including fish, molluscs, crustaceans and aquatic plants and implies some form of intervention in the rearing process to enhance production, such as regular stocking, feeding, protection from predators, etc. Farming also implies individual or <a href="/wiki/Corporate_farming" title="Corporate farming">corporate ownership</a> of the stock being cultivated."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> In practice it can take place in the sea or in freshwater, and be extensive or intensive. Whole bays, lakes or ponds may be devoted to aquaculture, or the farmed animal may be retained in cages (fish), <a href="/wiki/Artificial_reef" title="Artificial reef">artificial reefs</a>, racks or strings (shellfish). Fish and prawns can be cultivated in <a href="/wiki/Paddy_field" title="Paddy field">rice paddies</a>, either arriving naturally or being introduced, and both crops can be harvested together.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Fish_hatchery" title="Fish hatchery">Fish hatcheries</a> provide larval and juvenile fish, crustaceans and shellfish, for use in aquaculture systems. When large enough these are transferred to growing-on tanks and sold to fish farms to reach harvest size. Some species that are commonly raised in hatcheries include <a href="/wiki/Shrimp" title="Shrimp">shrimps</a>, <a href="/wiki/Prawn" title="Prawn">prawns</a>, <a href="/wiki/Salmon" title="Salmon">salmon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tilapia" title="Tilapia">tilapia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oyster" title="Oyster">oysters</a> and <a href="/wiki/Scallop" title="Scallop">scallops</a>. Similar facilities can be used to raise species with conservation needs to be released into the wild, or game fish for restocking waterways. Important aspects of husbandry at these early stages include selection of breeding stock, control of water quality and nutrition. In the wild, there is a massive amount of mortality at the nursery stage; farmers seek to minimise this while at the same time maximising growth rates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mosig_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mosig-70">[70]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Insects">Insects</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Animal_husbandry&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Insects">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bolikhamxay_Thabok_Crickets.JPG"><img alt="Crickets" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="640" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Bolikhamxay_Thabok_Crickets.JPG/220px-Bolikhamxay_Thabok_Crickets.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Bolikhamxay_Thabok_Crickets.JPG/330px-Bolikhamxay_Thabok_Crickets.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Bolikhamxay_Thabok_Crickets.JPG/440px-Bolikhamxay_Thabok_Crickets.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bolikhamxay_Thabok_Crickets.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Cricket_(insect)" title="Cricket (insect)">Crickets</a> being raised <a href="/wiki/Entomophagy" title="Entomophagy">for human consumption</a>, Thailand</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Beekeeping" title="Beekeeping">Beekeeping</a>, <a href="/wiki/Entomophagy" title="Entomophagy">Entomophagy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Insect_farming" title="Insect farming">Insect farming</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Sericulture" title="Sericulture">Sericulture</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Honey_bee" title="Honey bee">Bees</a> have been kept in hives since at least the <a href="/wiki/First_Dynasty_of_Egypt" title="First Dynasty of Egypt">First Dynasty of Egypt</a>, five thousand years ago,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> and man had been harvesting honey from the wild long before that. Fixed comb hives are used in many parts of the world and are made from any locally available material.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> In more advanced economies, where modern strains of domestic bee have been selected for docility and productiveness, various designs of hive are used which enable the combs to be removed for processing and extraction of honey. Quite apart from the <a href="/wiki/Honey" title="Honey">honey</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wax" title="Wax">wax</a> they produce, honey bees are important <a href="/wiki/Pollinator" title="Pollinator">pollinators</a> of crops and wild plants, and in many places hives are transported around the countryside to assist in pollination.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Sericulture" title="Sericulture">Sericulture</a>, the rearing of silkworms, was first adopted by the Chinese during the <a href="/wiki/Shang_dynasty" title="Shang dynasty">Shang dynasty</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> The only species farmed commercially is the domesticated <a href="/wiki/Bombyx_mori" title="Bombyx mori">silkmoth</a>. When it spins its <a href="/wiki/Pupa#Cocoon" title="Pupa">cocoon</a>, each larva produces an exceedingly long, slender thread of silk. The larvae feed on <a href="/wiki/Morus_alba" title="Morus alba">mulberry leaves</a> and in Europe, only one generation is normally raised each year as this is a deciduous tree. In China, Korea and Japan however, two generations are normal, and in the tropics, multiple generations are expected. Most production of silk occurs in the Far East, with a synthetic diet being used to rear the silkworms in Japan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hill_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hill-75">[75]</a></sup>
</p><p>Insects form part of the human diet in many cultures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carrington2010_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-Carrington2010-76">[76]</a></sup> In Thailand, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crickets" title="Crickets">crickets</a> are farmed for this purpose in the north of the country, and <a href="/wiki/Rhynchophorus_ferrugineus" title="Rhynchophorus ferrugineus">palm weevil</a> larvae in the south. The crickets are kept in pens, boxes or drawers and fed on commercial pelleted poultry food, while the palm weevil larvae live on <a href="/wiki/Cabbage_palm" title="Cabbage palm">cabbage palm</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sago_palm" title="Sago palm">sago palm</a> trees, which limits their production to areas where these trees grow.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bangkok_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bangkok-77">[77]</a></sup> Another delicacy of this region is the <a href="/wiki/Omphisa_fuscidentalis" title="Omphisa fuscidentalis">bamboo caterpillar</a>, and the best rearing and harvesting techniques in semi-natural habitats are being studied.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bangkok_77-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bangkok-77">[77]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Effects">Effects</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Animal_husbandry&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Effects">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p><span class="anchor" id="Environmental_Impact"></span>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Environmental_impact">Environmental impact</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Animal_husbandry&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Environmental impact">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Environmental_impact_of_livestock" title="Environmental impact of livestock">Environmental impact of livestock</a> and <a href="/wiki/Environmental_impact_of_meat_production" title="Environmental impact of meat production">Environmental impact of meat production</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bezerros_de_IATF.jpg"><img alt="Cattle" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1536" data-file-width="2048" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Bezerros_de_IATF.jpg/220px-Bezerros_de_IATF.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Bezerros_de_IATF.jpg/330px-Bezerros_de_IATF.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Bezerros_de_IATF.jpg/440px-Bezerros_de_IATF.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bezerros_de_IATF.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Livestock production requires large areas of land.</div></div></div>
<p>Animal husbandry has a significant impact on the world environment. Both production and consumption of animal products have increased rapidly. Over the past 50 years, meat production has trebled, whereas the production of dairy products doubled and that of eggs almost increased fourfold.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> Meanwhile, meat consumption has also nearly doubled worldwide. Within that increased overall consumption of meat, developing countries had a surge in meat consumption particularly in the portion of monogastric livestock.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> Being a part of the <a href="/wiki/Animal%E2%80%93industrial_complex" title="Animal–industrial complex">animal–industrial complex</a>, animal agriculture is the primary driver of climate change, ocean acidification, biodiversity loss, and of the crossing of almost every other planetary boundary, in addition to killing more than 60 billion non-human land animals annually.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> It is responsible for somewhere between 20 and 33% of the fresh water usage in the world,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> and livestock, and the production of feed for them, occupy about a third of the Earth's ice-free land.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> Livestock production is a contributing factor in species <a href="/wiki/Extinction" title="Extinction">extinction</a>, <a href="/wiki/Desertification" title="Desertification">desertification</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Habitat_destruction" title="Habitat destruction">habitat destruction</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> Animal agriculture contributes to species extinction in various ways and is the primary driver of the <a href="/wiki/Holocene_extinction" title="Holocene extinction">Holocene extinction</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WorldScientists_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-WorldScientists-85">[85]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBCIPBES2019_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-BBCIPBES2019-87">[87]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Machovina_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-Machovina-88">[88]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smithers_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-Smithers-89">[89]</a></sup> It is estimated that 70% of the agricultural land and 30% of the total land surface of the Earth is involved either directly or indirectly in animal agriculture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> Habitat is destroyed by clearing forests and converting land to grow feed crops and for animal grazing, while predators and herbivores are frequently targeted and hunted because of a perceived threat to livestock profits; for example, animal husbandry is responsible for up to 91% of the <a href="/wiki/Deforestation_of_the_Amazon_rainforest" title="Deforestation of the Amazon rainforest">deforestation in the Amazon region</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> In addition, livestock produce <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas" title="Greenhouse gas">greenhouse gases</a>. Cows produce some 570 million cubic metres of methane per day,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> that accounts for 35 to 40% of the overall <a href="/wiki/Methane_emissions" title="Methane emissions">methane emissions</a> of the planet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FAOshadow_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-FAOshadow-93">[93]</a></sup> Further, livestock production is responsible for 65% of all human-related emissions of <a href="/wiki/Nitrous_oxide" title="Nitrous oxide">nitrous oxide</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FAOshadow_93-1"><a href="#cite_note-FAOshadow-93">[93]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McAfee_McSorley_2010_60-1"><a href="#cite_note-McAfee_McSorley_2010-60">[60]</a></sup>
</p><p>As a result, ways of mitigating animal husbandry's environmental impact are being studied.
