Strategies include using <a href="/wiki/Biogas" title="Biogas">biogas</a> from manure,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> genetic selection,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup> immunization, rumen <a href="/wiki/Defaunation" title="Defaunation">defaunation</a>, outcompetition of methanogenic archaea with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Acetogens" title="Acetogens">acetogens</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> introduction of <a href="/wiki/Methanotroph" title="Methanotroph">methanotrophic bacteria</a> into the rumen,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> diet modification and grazing management, among others.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> It has been suggested that beef products finished in feedlot are less resource intensive than those pastured beef products.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> A diet change (with <i><a href="/wiki/Asparagopsis_taxiformis" title="Asparagopsis taxiformis">Asparagopsis taxiformis</a></i>) allowed for a reduction of up to 99% of methane production in an experimental study with three ruminants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Animal_welfare">Animal welfare</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Animal_husbandry&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Animal welfare">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Animal_welfare" title="Animal welfare">Animal welfare</a></div>
<p>Since the 18th century, people have become increasingly concerned about <a href="/wiki/Animal_welfare" title="Animal welfare">the welfare of farm animals</a>. Possible measures of welfare include <a href="/wiki/Longevity" title="Longevity">longevity</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ethology" title="Ethology">behavior</a>, <a href="/wiki/Physiology" title="Physiology">physiology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Reproduction" title="Reproduction">reproduction</a>, freedom from <a href="/wiki/Disease" title="Disease">disease</a>, and freedom from <a href="/wiki/Immunosuppression" title="Immunosuppression">immunosuppression</a>. Standards and laws for animal welfare have been created worldwide, broadly in line with the most widely held position in the western world, a form of <a href="/wiki/Utilitarianism" title="Utilitarianism">utilitarianism</a>: that it is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Moral_philosophy" title="Moral philosophy">morally acceptable</a> for humans to use non-human animals, provided that no unnecessary suffering is caused, and that the benefits to humans outweigh the costs to the livestock. An opposing view is that <a href="/wiki/Animal_rights" title="Animal rights">animals have rights</a>, should not be regarded as property, are not necessary to use, and should never be used by humans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Live_export" title="Live export">Live export</a> of animals has risen to meet increased global demand for livestock such as in the <a href="/wiki/Middle_East" title="Middle East">Middle East</a>. Animal rights activists have objected to long-distance transport of animals; one result was the banning of live exports from New Zealand in 2003.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/David_Nibert" title="David Nibert">David Nibert</a>, professor of sociology at <a href="/wiki/Wittenberg_University" title="Wittenberg University">Wittenberg University</a>, posits that, based on contemporary scholarship by ethologists and biologists about the sentience and intelligence of other animals, "we can assume that, for the most part, the other animals' experience of capture, enslavement, use, and slaying was one of suffering and violence." Much of this involved direct physical violence, but also structural violence as their systemic oppression and enslavement "resulted in their inability to meet their basic needs, the loss of self-determination, and the loss of opportunity to live in a natural way." He says that the remains of domesticated animals from thousands of years ago found during archeological excavations revealed numerous bone pathologies, which provide evidence of extreme suffering:
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Excavations from 8500 BCE revealed bone deformities in enslaved goats and cows and provided "some indication of stress, presumably due to the conditions in which these early <i>domestic</i> animals were kept." Remains of sheep and goats from the early Bronze Age show a marked decrease in bone thickness, reflecting calcium deficiencies "resulting from the combined effects of poor nutrition and intensive milking."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_culture">In culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Animal_husbandry&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: In culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Opening_of_the_budget;_-_or_-_John_Bull_giving_his_breeches_to_save_his_bacon_by_James_Gillray.jpg"><img alt="Cartoon of John Bull giving his breeches to save his bacon" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1785" data-file-width="2400" decoding="async" height="164" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Opening_of_the_budget%3B_-_or_-_John_Bull_giving_his_breeches_to_save_his_bacon_by_James_Gillray.jpg/220px-Opening_of_the_budget%3B_-_or_-_John_Bull_giving_his_breeches_to_save_his_bacon_by_James_Gillray.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Opening_of_the_budget%3B_-_or_-_John_Bull_giving_his_breeches_to_save_his_bacon_by_James_Gillray.jpg/330px-Opening_of_the_budget%3B_-_or_-_John_Bull_giving_his_breeches_to_save_his_bacon_by_James_Gillray.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Opening_of_the_budget%3B_-_or_-_John_Bull_giving_his_breeches_to_save_his_bacon_by_James_Gillray.jpg/440px-Opening_of_the_budget%3B_-_or_-_John_Bull_giving_his_breeches_to_save_his_bacon_by_James_Gillray.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Opening_of_the_budget;_-_or_-_John_Bull_giving_his_breeches_to_save_his_bacon_by_James_Gillray.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Opening of the budget; – or – <a href="/wiki/John_Bull" title="John Bull">John Bull</a> giving his <a href="/wiki/Breeches" title="Breeches">breeches</a> to <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/save_someone%27s_bacon" title="wikt:save someone's bacon">save his bacon</a></i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[note 1]</a></sup> by <a href="/wiki/James_Gillray" title="James Gillray">James Gillray</a> (d. 1815)</div></div></div>
<p>Since the 18th century, the farmer <a href="/wiki/John_Bull" title="John Bull">John Bull</a> has represented <a href="/wiki/English_national_identity" title="English national identity">English national identity</a>, first in <a href="/wiki/John_Arbuthnot" title="John Arbuthnot">John Arbuthnot</a>'s political satires, and soon afterwards in cartoons by <a href="/wiki/James_Gillray" title="James Gillray">James Gillray</a> and others including <a href="/wiki/John_Tenniel" title="John Tenniel">John Tenniel</a>. He likes food, beer, dogs, horses, and <a href="/wiki/Field_sports" title="Field sports">country sports</a>; he is practical and down to earth, and anti-intellectual.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[114]</a></sup>
</p><p>Farm animals are widespread in books and songs for children; the reality of animal husbandry is often distorted, softened, or idealized, giving children an almost entirely fictitious account of farm life. The books often depict happy animals free to roam in attractive countryside, a picture completely at odds with the realities of the impersonal, mechanized activities involved in modern intensive farming.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hoult_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hoult-116">[115]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_tale_of_Pigling_Bland_pg_13.jpg"><img alt="Illustration of dressed pigs" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1129" data-file-width="933" decoding="async" height="206" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/The_tale_of_Pigling_Bland_pg_13.jpg/170px-The_tale_of_Pigling_Bland_pg_13.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/The_tale_of_Pigling_Bland_pg_13.jpg/255px-The_tale_of_Pigling_Bland_pg_13.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/The_tale_of_Pigling_Bland_pg_13.jpg/340px-The_tale_of_Pigling_Bland_pg_13.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_tale_of_Pigling_Bland_pg_13.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Dressed pigs in <a href="/wiki/Beatrix_Potter" title="Beatrix Potter">Beatrix Potter</a>'s 1913 <i><a href="/wiki/The_Tale_of_Pigling_Bland" title="The Tale of Pigling Bland">The Tale of Pigling Bland</a></i></div></div></div>
<p>Pigs, for example, appear in several of <a href="/wiki/Beatrix_Potter" title="Beatrix Potter">Beatrix Potter</a>'s "little books", as Piglet in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/A.A._Milne" title="A.A. Milne">A.A. Milne</a>'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Winnie_the_Pooh" title="Winnie the Pooh">Winnie the Pooh</a> stories, and somewhat more darkly (with a hint of animals going to slaughter) as Babe in <a href="/wiki/Dick_King-Smith" title="Dick King-Smith">Dick King-Smith</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_Sheep-Pig" title="The Sheep-Pig">The Sheep-Pig</a></i>, and as Wilbur in <a href="/wiki/E._B._White" title="E. B. White">E. B. White</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Charlotte%27s_Web" title="Charlotte's Web">Charlotte's Web</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[116]</a></sup> Pigs tend to be "bearers of cheerfulness, good humour and innocence". Many of these books are completely <a href="/wiki/Anthropomorphism" title="Anthropomorphism">anthropomorphic</a>, dressing farm animals in clothes and having them walk on two legs, live in houses, and perform human activities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hoult_116-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hoult-116">[115]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Children%27s_music" title="Children's music">children's song</a> "<a href="/wiki/Old_MacDonald_Had_a_Farm" title="Old MacDonald Had a Farm">Old MacDonald Had a Farm</a>" describes a farmer named MacDonald and the various animals he keeps, celebrating the noises they each make.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[117]</a></sup>
</p><p>Many urban children experience animal husbandry for the first time at a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Petting_farm" title="Petting farm">petting farm</a>; in Britain, some five million people a year visit a farm of some kind. This presents some risk of <a href="/wiki/Zoonosis#Farming,_ranching_and_animal_husbandry" title="Zoonosis">infection</a>, especially if children handle animals and then fail to <a href="/wiki/Hygiene" title="Hygiene">wash their hands</a>; a strain of <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/E._coli" title="E. coli">E. coli</a></i> <a href="/wiki/Infection" title="Infection">infected</a> 93 people who had visited a British interactive farm in an outbreak in 2009.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[118]</a></sup> Historic farms such as those in the United States offer farmstays and "a carefully curated version of farming to those willing to pay for it",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Searle2014_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-Searle2014-120">[119]</a></sup> sometimes giving visitors a romanticised image of a <a href="/wiki/Pastoral" title="Pastoral">pastoral</a> idyll from an unspecified time in the pre-industrial past.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Searle2014_120-1"><a href="#cite_note-Searle2014-120">[119]</a></sup>
</p>

<li id="cite_note-McTavish-6"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-McTavish_6-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation journal cs1" id="CITEREFMcTavishDeckerSchnabelTaylor2013">McTavish, E.J.; Decker, J.E.; Schnabel, R.D.; Taylor, J.F.; Hillis, D.M. title="Bibcode (identifier)">Bibcode</a>:<a class="external text" href="https://ui.adsabs.harvard.edu/abs/2013PNAS..110E1398M" rel="nofollow">2013PNAS..110E1398M</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doi_(identifier)" title="Doi (identifier)">doi</a>:<span class="cs1-lock-free" title="Freely accessible"><a class="external text" href="https://doi.org/10.1073%2Fpnas.1303367110" rel="nofollow">10.1073/pnas.1303367110</a></span>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PMC_(identifier)" title="PMC (identifier)">PMC</a> <span class="cs1-lock-free" title="Freely accessible"><a class="external text" href="//www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC3625352" rel="nofollow">3625352</a></span>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PMID_(identifier)" title="PMID (identifier)">PMID</a> <a class="external text" href="//pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/23530234" rel="nofollow">23530234</a>. <q>Cattle were independently domesticated from the aurochs, a wild bovine species, in the vicinity of the current countries of Turkey and Pakistan ∼10,000 y ago.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=PNAS&amp;rft.atitle=New+World+cattle+show+ancestry+from+multiple+independent+domestication+events&amp;rft.volume=110&amp;rft.issue=15&amp;rft.pages=1398-1406&amp;rft.date=2013&amp;rft_id=%2F%2Fwww.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov%2Fpmc%2Farticles%2FPMC3625352%23id-name%3DPMC&amp;rft_id=info%3Apmid%2F23530234&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1073%2Fpnas.1303367110&amp;rft_id=info%3Abibcode%2F2013PNAS..110E1398M&amp;rft.aulast=McTavish&amp;rft.aufirst=E.J.&amp;rft.au=Decker%2C+J.E.&amp;rft.au=Schnabel%2C+R.D.&amp;rft.au=Taylor%2C+J.F.&amp;rft.au=Hillis%2C+D.M.&amp;rft_id=%2F%2Fwww.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov%2Fpmc%2Farticles%2FPMC3625352&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AAnimal+husbandry"></span></span>
